      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/12542.Bernard_Cornwell">About Bernard Cornwell</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/12542.Bernard_Cornwell"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1504578807p3/12542.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/12542.Bernard_Cornwell">Bernard Cornwell
</a></div>
<div class='goodreadsAuthorBadge'></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
13,028
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer9539870063869537875">Cornwell was born in London in 1944. His father was a Canadian airman, and his mother, who was English, a member of the Women's Auxiliary Air Force. He was adopted and brought up in Essex by the Wiggins family, who were members of the Peculiar People, a strict Protestant sect who banned frivolity of all kinds and even medicine. After he left them, he changed his name to his birth mother's maiden n</span>
  <span id="freeText9539870063869537875" style="display:none">Cornwell was born in London in 1944. His father was a Canadian airman, and his mother, who was English, a member of the Women's Auxiliary Air Force. He was adopted and brought up in Essex by the Wiggins family, who were members of the Peculiar People, a strict Protestant sect who banned frivolity of all kinds and even medicine. After he left them, he changed his name to his birth mother's maiden name, Cornwell.<br /><br />Cornwell was sent away to Monkton Combe School, attended the University of London, and after graduating, worked as a teacher. He attempted to enlist in the British armed services at least three times but was rejected on the grounds of myopia.<br /><br />He then joined BBC's Nationwide and was promoted to become head of current affairs at BBC Northern Ireland. He then joined Thames Television as editor of Thames News. He relocated to the United States in 1980 after marrying an American. Unable to get a green card, he started writing novels, as this did not require a work permit.<br /><br />As a child, Cornwell loved the novels of C.S. Forester, chronicling the adventures of fictional British naval officer Horatio Hornblower during the Napoleonic Wars, and was surprised to find there were no such novels following Lord Wellington's campaign on land. Motivated by the need to support himself in the U.S. through writing, Cornwell decided to write such a series. He named his chief protagonist Richard Sharpe, a rifleman involved in most major battles of the Peninsular War.<br /><br />Cornwell wanted to start the series with the Siege of Badajoz but decided instead to start with a couple of "warm-up" novels. These were <i>Sharpe's Eagle</i> and <i>Sharpe's Gold</i>, both published in 1981. <i>Sharpe's Eagle</i> was picked up by a publisher, and Cornwell got a three-book deal. He went on to tell the story of Badajoz in his third Sharpe novel, <i>Sharpe's Company</i>, published in 1982.<br /><br />Cornwell and wife Judy co-wrote a series of novels, published under the pseudonym "Susannah Kells". These were <i>A Crowning Mercy</i>, published in 1983, <i>Fallen Angels</i> in 1984, and <i>Coat of Arms</i> (aka <i>The Aristocrats</i>) in 1986. (Cornwell's strict Protestant upbringing informed the background of <i>A Crowning Mercy</i>, which took place during the English Civil War.) In 1987, he also published <i>Redcoat</i>, an American Revolutionary War novel set in Philadelphia during its 1777 occupation by the British.<br /><br />After publishing eight books in his ongoing Sharpe series, Cornwell was approached by a production company interested in adapting them for television. The producers asked him to write a prequel to give them a starting point to the series. They also requested that the story feature a large role for Spanish characters to secure co-funding from Spain. The result was <i>Sharpe’s Rifles</i>, published in 1987, and a series of Sharpe television films staring Sean Bean.<br /><br />A series of contemporary thrillers with sailing as a background and common themes followed: <i>Wildtrack</i> published in 1988, <i>Sea Lord</i> (aka <i>Killer's Wake</i>) in 1989, <i>Crackdown</i> in 1990, <i>Stormchild</i> in 1991, and <i>Scoundrel</i>, a political thriller, in 1992.<br /><br />In June 2006, Cornwell was made an Officer of the Order of the British Empire in the Queen's 80th Birthday Honours List.<br /><br />Cornwell's latest work, <i>Azincourt</i>, was released in the UK in October 2008. The protagonist is an archer who participates in the Battle of Agincourt, another devastating defeat suffered by the French in the Hundred Years War. However, Cornwell has stated that it will not be about Thomas of Hookton from The Grail Quest or any of his relatives.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9539870063869537875" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

He flees Wessex, going back north to seek revenge for the killing of his foster father and to rescue his stepsister, captured in the same raid. He needs to find his old enemy, Kjartan, a renegade Danish lord who lurks in the formidable stronghold of Dunholm. Uhtred arrives in the north to discover rebellion, chaos and fear. His only ally is Hild, a West Saxon nun fleeing her calling, and his best hope is his sword, with which he has made a formidable reputation as a warrior. He will need the assistance of other warriors if he is to attack Dunholm and he finds Guthred, a slave who believes he is a king. He takes him across the Pennines to where a desperate alliance of fanatical Christians and beleaguered Danes form a new army to confront the terrible Viking lords who rule Northumbria. \'The Lords of the North\' is a powerful story of betrayal, romance and struggle, set in an England of turmoil, upheaval and glory.
